He left the administration's industrial policy in the hands of his activist Secretary of Commerce, <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">Herbert Hoover</a>, who energetically used government auspices to promote business efficiency and develop airlines and radio.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerrell64.E2.80.9365_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerrell64.E2.80.9365-109"><span>[</span>106<span>]</span></a></sup> With the exception of favoring increased tariffs, Coolidge disdained regulation, and demonstrated this by appointing commissioners to the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Trade_Commission" title="Federal Trade Commission">Federal Trade Commission</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Interstate_Commerce_Commission" title="Interstate Commerce Commission">Interstate Commerce Commission</a> who did little to restrict the activities of businesses under their jurisdiction.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerrell66.E2.80.9372Sobel318_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerrell66.E2.80.9372Sobel318-110"><span>[</span>107<span>]</span></a></sup> The regulatory state under Coolidge was, as one biographer described it, "thin to the point of invisibility."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerrell72_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerrell72-111"><span>[</span>108<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Coolidge's economic policy has often been misquoted as "generally speaking, the business of the American people is business" (full quotation at right). Some have criticized Coolidge as an adherent of the <a href="/wiki/Laissez-faire" title="Laissez-faire">laissez-faire</a> ideology, which they claim led to the <a href="/wiki/Great_Depression" title="Great Depression">Great Depression</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerrell207_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerrell207-112"><span>[</span>109<span>]</span></a></sup> On the other hand, historian <a href="/wiki/Robert_Sobel" title="Robert Sobel">Robert Sobel</a> offers some context based on Coolidge's sense of <a href="/wiki/Federalism" title="Federalism">federalism</a>: "As Governor of Massachusetts, Coolidge supported wages and hours legislation, opposed <a href="/wiki/Child_labor" title="Child labor" class="mw-redirect">child labor</a>, imposed economic controls during <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, favored safety measures in factories, and even worker representation on corporate boards. Did he support these measures while president? No, because in the 1920s, such matters were considered the responsibilities of state and local governments."<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113"><span>[</span>d<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenberg47Ferrell62_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenberg47Ferrell62-114"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Taxation_and_government_spending">Taxation and government spending</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Taxation and government spending">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Coolidge's taxation policy was that of his Secretary of the Treasury, <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Mellon" title="Andrew Mellon">Andrew Mellon</a>, the ideal that "scientific taxation"——lower taxes——actually increase rather than decrease government receipts.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel310.E2.80.9311Greenberg127.E2.80.9329_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel310.E2.80.9311Greenberg127.E2.80.9329-115"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup> Congress agreed, and the taxes were reduced in Coolidge's term.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel310.E2.80.9311Greenberg127.E2.80.9329_115-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel310.E2.80.9311Greenberg127.E2.80.9329-115"><span>[</span>111<span>]</span></a></sup> In addition to these tax cuts, Coolidge proposed reductions in federal expenditures and retiring some of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_public_debt" title="United States public debt" class="mw-redirect">federal debt</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel310.E2.80.9311Fuess382.E2.80.9383_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel310.E2.80.9311Fuess382.E2.80.9383-116"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup> Coolidge's ideas were shared by the Republicans in Congress, and in 1924, Congress passed the <a href="/wiki/Revenue_Act_of_1924" title="Revenue Act of 1924">Revenue Act of 1924</a>, which reduced income tax rates and eliminated all income taxation for some two million people.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel310.E2.80.9311Fuess382.E2.80.9383_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel310.E2.80.9311Fuess382.E2.80.9383-116"><span>[</span>112<span>]</span></a></sup> They reduced taxes again by passing the Revenue Acts of <a href="/wiki/Revenue_Act_of_1926" title="Revenue Act of 1926">1926</a> and <a href="/wiki/Revenue_Act_of_1928" title="Revenue Act of 1928">1928</a>, all the while continuing to keep spending down so as to reduce the overall federal debt.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerrell170_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerrell170-117"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup> By 1927, only the wealthiest 2% of taxpayers paid any federal income tax.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerrell170_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerrell170-117"><span>[</span>113<span>]</span></a></sup> Although federal spending remained flat during Coolidge's administration, allowing one-fourth of the federal debt to be retired, state and local governments saw considerable growth, surpassing the federal budget in 1927.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerrell174_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerrell174-118"><span>[</span>114<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Opposition_to_farm_subsidies">Opposition to farm subsidies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Opposition to farm subsidies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coolidge-Dawes.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Coolidge-Dawes.jpg/170px-Coolidge-Dawes.jpg" width="170" height="351" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Coolidge-Dawes.jpg/255px-Coolidge-Dawes.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Coolidge-Dawes.jpg/340px-Coolidge-Dawes.jpg 2x" data-file-width="391" data-file-height="808" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coolidge-Dawes.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Coolidge with his Vice President, <a href="/wiki/Charles_G._Dawes" title="Charles G. Dawes">Charles G. Dawes</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Perhaps the most contentious issue of Coolidge's presidency was relief for farmers. Some in Congress proposed a bill designed to fight falling agricultural prices by allowing the federal government to purchase crops to sell abroad at lower prices.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerrell84McCoy234.E2.80.9335_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerrell84McCoy234.E2.80.9335-119"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup> Agriculture Secretary <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cantwell_Wallace" title="Henry Cantwell Wallace">Henry C. Wallace</a> and other administration officials favored the bill when it was introduced in 1924, but rising prices convinced many in Congress that the bill was unnecessary, and it was defeated just before the elections that year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy235_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy235-120"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1926, with farm prices falling once more, Senator <a href="/wiki/Charles_L._McNary" title="Charles L. McNary">Charles L. McNary</a> and Representative <a href="/wiki/Gilbert_N._Haugen" title="Gilbert N. Haugen">Gilbert N. Haugen</a>—both Republicans—proposed the <a href="/wiki/McNary%E2%80%93Haugen_Farm_Relief_Bill" title="McNary–Haugen Farm Relief Bill">McNary–Haugen Farm Relief Bill</a>. The bill proposed a federal farm board that would purchase surplus production in high-yield years and hold it (when feasible) for later sale or sell it abroad.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess383.E2.80.9384_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess383.E2.80.9384-121"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup> Coolidge opposed McNary-Haugen, declaring that agriculture must stand "on an independent business basis," and said that "government control cannot be divorced from political control."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess383.E2.80.9384_121-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess383.E2.80.9384-121"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup> He favored instead <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">Herbert Hoover</a>'s proposal to modernize agriculture to create profits, instead of manipulating prices. Secretary Mellon wrote a letter denouncing the McNary-Haugen measure as unsound and likely to cause inflation, and it was defeated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel327_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel327-122"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After McNary-Haugen's defeat, Coolidge supported a less radical measure, the Curtis-Crisp Act, which would have created a federal board to lend money to farm co-operatives in times of surplus; the bill did not pass.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel327_122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel327-122"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup> In February 1927, Congress took up the McNary-Haugen bill again, this time narrowly passing it and Coolidge vetoed it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess388Ferrell93_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess388Ferrell93-123"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> In his veto message, he expressed the belief that the bill would do nothing to help farmers, benefiting only exporters and expanding the federal bureaucracy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel331_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel331-124"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup> Congress did not override the veto, but it passed the bill again in May 1928 by an increased majority; again, Coolidge vetoed it.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess388Ferrell93_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess388Ferrell93-123"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> "Farmers never have made much money," said Coolidge, the Vermont farmer's son, "I do not believe we can do much about it."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerrell86_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerrell86-125"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Flood_control">Flood control</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Flood control">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Coolidge has often been criticized for his actions during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Mississippi_Flood_of_1927" title="Great Mississippi Flood of 1927">Great Mississippi Flood of 1927</a>, the worst natural disaster to hit the Gulf Coast until <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Katrina" title="Hurricane Katrina">Hurricane Katrina</a> in 2005.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel315Barry286.E2.80.9387Greenberg132.E2.80.9335_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel315Barry286.E2.80.9387Greenberg132.E2.80.9335-126"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> Although he did eventually name Secretary Hoover to a commission in charge of flood relief, Coolidge's lack of interest in federal flood control has been criticized.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel315Barry286.E2.80.9387Greenberg132.E2.80.9335_126-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel315Barry286.E2.80.9387Greenberg132.E2.80.9335-126"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> Coolidge did not believe that personally visiting the region after the floods would accomplish anything, but it would be seen only as political grandstanding. He also did not want to incur the federal spending that flood control would require; he believed property owners should bear much of the cost.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy330.E2.80.9331_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy330.E2.80.9331-127"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup> On the other hand, Congress wanted a bill that would place the federal government completely in charge of flood mitigation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarry372.E2.80.9374_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarry372.E2.80.9374-128"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup> When Congress passed a compromise measure in 1928, Coolidge declined to take credit for it and signed the bill in private on May 15.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenberg135_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenberg135-129"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Civil_rights">Civil rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Civil rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1924_Indian_Citizenship_Act.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/1924_Indian_Citizenship_Act.jpg/220px-1924_Indian_Citizenship_Act.jpg" width="220" height="168" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/1924_Indian_Citizenship_Act.jpg/330px-1924_Indian_Citizenship_Act.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/1924_Indian_Citizenship_Act.jpg/440px-1924_Indian_Citizenship_Act.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3321" data-file-height="2532" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1924_Indian_Citizenship_Act.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Osage_Nation" title="Osage Nation">Osage</a> men with Coolidge after he signed the bill granting Native Americans U.S. citizenship.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Coolidge spoke in favor of the civil rights of <a href="/wiki/African-American" title="African-American" class="mw-redirect">African-Americans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church_in_the_United_States" title="Catholic Church in the United States">Catholics</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel250McCoy328.E2.80.9329_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel250McCoy328.E2.80.9329-130"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup> saying in his first <a href="/wiki/State_of_the_Union_address" title="State of the Union address" class="mw-redirect">State of the Union address</a> that the rights of the former were "just as sacred as those of any other citizen" under the U.S. Constitution and that it was a "public and a private duty to protect those rights."<sup id="cite_ref-1923SOTU_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1923SOTU-131"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup> He appointed no known members of the <a href="/wiki/Ku_Klux_Klan" title="Ku Klux Klan">Ku Klux Klan</a> to office; indeed, the Klan lost most of its influence during his term.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFelzenberg83.E2.80.9396_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFelzenberg83.E2.80.9396-132"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup> His administration commissioned studies to improve programs for Native Americans.</p>
<p>Coolidge repeatedly called for laws to prohibit <a href="/wiki/Lynching_in_the_United_States" title="Lynching in the United States">lynching</a>, saying in his 1923 State of the Union address that it was a "hideous crime" of which African-Americans were "by no means the sole sufferers," but consisted of the "majority of the victims."<sup id="cite_ref-1923SOTU_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1923SOTU-131"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup> However, most Congressional attempts to pass this legislation were filibustered by Southern Democrats.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel249.E2.80.9350_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel249.E2.80.9350-133"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup> Coolidge appointed some African-Americans to federal office; he retained Harding's choice of <a href="/wiki/Walter_L._Cohen" title="Walter L. Cohen">Walter L. Cohen</a> of <a href="/wiki/New_Orleans,_Louisiana" title="New Orleans, Louisiana" class="mw-redirect">New Orleans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a>, as the comptroller of customs and offered Cohen the post of minister to <a href="/wiki/Liberia" title="Liberia">Liberia</a>, which the businessman declined.</p>
<p>On June 2, 1924, Coolidge signed the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Citizenship_Act_of_1924" title="Indian Citizenship Act of 1924" class="mw-redirect">Indian Citizenship Act</a>, which granted U.S. citizenship to all <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_the_Americas" title="Indigenous peoples of the Americas">American Indians</a>, while permitting them to retain tribal land and cultural rights. However, the act was unclear on whether the federal government or the tribal leaders retained tribal sovereignty.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDeloria91_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeloria91-134"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup> His administration appointed the Committee of One Hundred, a reform panel to examine federal institutions and programs dealing with Indian nations. This committee recommended that the government conduct an in-depth investigation into reservation life (health, education, economics, justice, civil rights, etc.). This was commissioned through the Department of Interior and conducted by the <a href="/wiki/Brookings_Institution" title="Brookings Institution">Brookings Institution</a>, resulting in the groundbreaking <i><a href="/wiki/Meriam_Report" title="Meriam Report">Meriam Report</a></i> of 1928.</p>
<p>A few days later, on June 6, 1924, Coolidge delivered a commencement address at Washington, D.C.'s <a href="/wiki/Howard_University" title="Howard University">Howard University</a>, in which he thanked and commended African-Americans for their rapid advances in education and their contributions to U.S. society over the years, as well as their eagerness to render their services as soldiers in <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a>, all while being faced with discrimination and prejudices at home:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">propaganda of prejudice and hatred</a> which sought to keep the colored men from supporting the national cause completely failed. The black man showed himself the same kind of citizen, moved by the same kind of patriotism, as the white man. They were tempted, but not one betrayed <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">his country</a>. ... They came home with many decorations and their conduct repeatedly won high commendation from both American and European commanders. ... No part of the community responded more willingly, more generously, more unqualifiedly, to the demand for special extraordinary exertion, than did the members of the Negro race. Whether in the military service, or in the vast mobilization of industrial resources which the war required, the Negro did his part precisely as did the white man. He drew no color line when patriotism made its call upon him. He gave precisely as his white fellow citizens gave, to the limit of resources and abilities, to help the general cause. Thus the American Negro established his right to the gratitude and appreciation which the Nation has been glad to accord.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoolidge192631-36_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoolidge192631-36-135"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>In August 1924, Coolidge responded to a letter from a New York man that claimed that the United States was a "white man's country" and that African-Americans should not be allowed to hold elected office as a result. Echoing his June 1924 speech at Howard University, Coolidge refuted the man's statement, saying that African-Americans were "just as truly citizens" of the United States "as are any others," and commended the service of black U.S. soldiers during World War I:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>I was amazed to receive such a letter. During the war 500,000 colored men and boys were called up under the draft, not one of whom sought to evade it. [As president, I am] one who feels a responsibility for living up to the traditions and maintaining the principles of the Republican Party. Our Constitution guarantees equal rights to all our citizens, without discrimination on account of race or color. I have taken my oath to support that Constitution. It is the source of your rights and my rights. I propose to regard it, and administer it, as the source of the rights of all the people, whatever their belief or race. A colored man is precisely as much entitled to submit his candidacy in a party primary, as is any other citizen. The decision must be made by the constituents to whom he offers himself, and by nobody else.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoolidge192671-72_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoolidge192671-72-136"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Speaking before a group of naturalized Americans at the White House in October 1924, Coolidge stressed tolerance of differences as an American value and thanked immigrants for their contributions to U.S. society, saying that they have "contributed much to making our country what it is." He stated that although the diversity of peoples was a detrimental source of conflict and tension in Europe, it was peculiar for the United States that it was a "harmonious" benefit for the country. Coolidge further stated the United States should assist and help immigrants who come to the country, and urged immigrants to reject "<a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">race hatreds</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Bigotry" title="Bigotry">prejudices</a>":</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>Among these I should place, first, the broadly tolerant attitude that has been a characteristic of this country. I use the word in its most inclusive sense, to cover tolerance of religious opinion, tolerance in politics, tolerance in social relationships; in general, the liberal attitude of every citizen toward his fellows. ... As a Nation, our first duty must be to those who are already our inhabitants, whether native or immigrants. To them we owe an especial and a weighty obligation. They came to us with stout hearts and high hopes of bettering their estate. They have contributed much to making our country what it is. ... They cannot help other nations by bringing old world race prejudices and race hatreds into action here. In fact, they can best help other countries by scrupulously avoiding any such motives.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoolidge1926159-156_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoolidge1926159-156-137"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>In December 1924, Coolidge delivered his second State of the Union address, in which he commended African-Americans for their advances, and stressed that their constitutional rights should be respected and protected:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>These developments have brought about a very remarkable improvement in the condition of the negro race. Gradually, but surely, with the almost universal sympathy of those among whom they live, the colored people are working out their own destiny. I firmly believe that it is better for all concerned that they should be cheerfully accorded their full constitutional rights, that they should be protected from all of those impositions to which, from their position, they naturally fall a prey, especially from the crime of lynching and that they should receive every encouragement to become full partakers in all the blessings of our common American citizenship.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>In a May 1926 speech delivered at Arlington to commemorate the U.S. military, Coolidge praised the linguistic, ethnic, and religious diversity of the U.S. population, citing it as an example of <a href="/wiki/American_exceptionalism" title="American exceptionalism">American exceptionalism</a>. He spoke out against "<a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">race hatred</a>" and "<a href="/wiki/Religious_intolerance" title="Religious intolerance">religious intolerance</a>," saying that engaging in such actions would be an "injury" and not a "benefit" to the country:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>We are situated differently in this respect from any other country. All the other great powers have a comparatively homogeneous population, close kindred in race and blood and speech, and commonly little divided in religious beliefs. Our great Nation is made up of the strong and virile pioneering stock of nearly all the countries of the world. We have a variety of race and language and religious belief. ... race hatred, religious intolerance, and disregard of equal rights ... are ... a positive injury.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoolidge1926429-437_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoolidge1926429-437-139"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policy">Foreign policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Foreign policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ccoolidge.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Ccoolidge.jpeg/220px-Ccoolidge.jpeg" width="220" height="244" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Ccoolidge.jpeg/330px-Ccoolidge.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/64/Ccoolidge.jpeg/440px-Ccoolidge.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="2707" data-file-height="3000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ccoolidge.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Official portrait of Calvin Coolidge</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although not an isolationist, Coolidge was reluctant to enter into foreign alliances.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel342_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel342-140"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup> He considered the 1920 Republican victory as a rejection of the <a href="/wiki/Woodrow_Wilson" title="Woodrow Wilson">Wilsonian</a> position that the United States should join the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy184.E2.80.9385_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy184.E2.80.9385-141"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup> While not completely opposed to the idea, Coolidge believed the League, as then constituted, did not serve American interests, and he did not advocate membership.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy184.E2.80.9385_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy184.E2.80.9385-141"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup> He spoke in favor of the United States joining the <a href="/wiki/Permanent_Court_of_International_Justice" title="Permanent Court of International Justice">Permanent Court of International Justice</a>, provided that the nation would not be bound by advisory decisions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy360_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy360-142"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1926, the Senate eventually approved joining the Court (with <a href="/wiki/Reservation_(law)" title="Reservation (law)">reservations</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy363_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy363-143"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup> The League of Nations accepted the reservations, but it suggested some modifications of their own.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenberg114.E2.80.9316_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenberg114.E2.80.9316-144"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup> The Senate failed to act; the United States never joined the World Court.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenberg114.E2.80.9316_144-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenberg114.E2.80.9316-144"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Coolidge's primary initiative was the <a href="/wiki/Kellogg%E2%80%93Briand_Pact" title="Kellogg–Briand Pact">Kellogg–Briand Pact</a> of 1928, named for Coolidge's Secretary of State, <a href="/wiki/Frank_B._Kellogg" title="Frank B. Kellogg">Frank B. Kellogg</a>, and French foreign minister <a href="/wiki/Aristide_Briand" title="Aristide Briand">Aristide Briand</a>. The treaty, ratified in 1929, committed signatories—the United States, the United Kingdom, France, Germany, Italy, and Japan—to "renounce war, as an instrument of national policy in their relations with one another."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess421.E2.80.9323_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess421.E2.80.9323-145"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup> The treaty did not achieve its intended result—the outlawry of war—but it did provide the founding principle for international law after <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy380.E2.80.9381Greenberg123.E2.80.9324_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy380.E2.80.9381Greenberg123.E2.80.9324-146"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Coolidge continued the previous administration's policy of withholding recognition of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy181_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy181-147"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup> He also continued the United States' support for the elected government of Mexico against the <a href="/wiki/Cristero_War" title="Cristero War">rebels</a> there, lifting the arms embargo on that country.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy178.E2.80.9379_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy178.E2.80.9379-148"><span>[</span>144<span>]</span></a></sup> He sent <a href="/wiki/Dwight_Morrow" title="Dwight Morrow">Dwight Morrow</a> to Mexico as the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Ambassador_to_Mexico" title="United States Ambassador to Mexico">American ambassador</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel349_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel349-149"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a></sup> Coolidge represented the United States at the Pan American Conference in <a href="/wiki/Havana" title="Havana">Havana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a>, making him the only sitting President to visit the country. The United States' occupation of <a href="/wiki/United_States_occupation_of_Nicaragua" title="United States occupation of Nicaragua">Nicaragua</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_occupation_of_Haiti" title="United States occupation of Haiti">Haiti</a> continued under his administration, but Coolidge withdrew American troops from the <a href="/wiki/1916_United_States_occupation_of_the_Dominican_Republic" title="1916 United States occupation of the Dominican Republic" class="mw-redirect">Dominican Republic</a> in 1924.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess414.E2.80.9317Ferrell122.E2.80.9323_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess414.E2.80.9317Ferrell122.E2.80.9323-150"><span>[</span>146<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1928_election">1928 election</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: 1928 election">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1928" title="United States presidential election, 1928">United States presidential election, 1928</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
<div id="mwe_player_0" class="PopUpMediaTransform" style="width:220px;" videopayload="&lt;div class=&quot;mediaContainer&quot; style=&quot;position:relative;display:block;width:480px&quot;&gt;&lt;video id=&quot;mwe_player_1&quot; Collection of video clips of the president</div>

<p>In the summer of 1927, Coolidge vacationed in the <a href="/wiki/Black_Hills" title="Black Hills">Black Hills</a> of <a href="/wiki/South_Dakota" title="South Dakota">South Dakota</a>, where he engaged in horseback riding and fly fishing and attended <a href="/wiki/Rodeo" title="Rodeo">rodeos</a>. He made <a href="/wiki/Custer_State_Park" title="Custer State Park">Custer State Park</a> his "summer White House." While on vacation, Coolidge surprisingly issued his terse statement that he would not seek a second full term as president: "<a href="/wiki/I_do_not_choose_to_run" title="I do not choose to run">I do not choose to run for President in 1928</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel370_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel370-151"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a></sup> After allowing the reporters to take that in, Coolidge elaborated. "If I take another term, I will be in the White House till 1933&#160;… Ten years in Washington is longer than any other man has had it—too long!"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhite361_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhite361-152"><span>[</span>148<span>]</span></a></sup> In his memoirs, Coolidge explained his decision not to run: "The Presidential office takes a heavy toll of those who occupy it and those who are dear to them. While we should not refuse to spend and be spent in the service of our country, it is hazardous to attempt what we feel is beyond our strength to accomplish."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECoolidge1929239_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECoolidge1929239-153"><span>[</span>149<span>]</span></a></sup> After leaving office, he and Grace returned to Northampton, where he wrote his memoirs. The Republicans retained the White House in 1928 in the person of Coolidge's <a href="/wiki/United_States_Secretary_of_Commerce" title="United States Secretary of Commerce">Secretary of Commerce</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">Herbert Hoover</a>. Coolidge had been reluctant to endorse Hoover as his successor; on one occasion he remarked that "for six years that man has given me unsolicited advice—all of it bad."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFerrell195_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFerrell195-154"><span>[</span>150<span>]</span></a></sup> Even so, Coolidge had no desire to split the party by publicly opposing the nomination of the popular commerce secretary.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy390.E2.80.9391Wilson122.E2.80.9323_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy390.E2.80.9391Wilson122.E2.80.9323-155"><span>[</span>151<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cabinet">Cabinet</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Cabinet">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coolidge_Cabinet.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Coolidge_Cabinet.jpg/400px-Coolidge_Cabinet.jpg" width="400" height="302" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Coolidge_Cabinet.jpg/600px-Coolidge_Cabinet.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Coolidge_Cabinet.jpg/800px-Coolidge_Cabinet.jpg 2x" data-file-width="867" data-file-height="655" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">

</div>

<th style=""><a href="/wiki/William_M._Jardine" title="William M. Jardine" class="mw-redirect">William M. Jardine</a></th>
<td>1925–1929</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td colspan="3" style="background: #D1D1D1"></td>
</tr>

<th style=""><a href="/wiki/Herbert_Hoover" title="Herbert Hoover">Herbert Hoover</a></th>
<td>1923–1928</td>

<th style=""><a href="/wiki/James_J._Davis" title="James J. Davis">James J. Davis</a></th>
<td>1923–1929</td>
</tr>
</table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Judicial_appointments">Judicial appointments</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Judicial appointments">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Coolidge_judicial_appointments" title="Calvin Coolidge judicial appointments" class="mw-redirect">Calvin Coolidge judicial appointments</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Chief_Justice_Harlan_Fiske_Stone_photograph_circa_1927-1932.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Chief_Justice_Harlan_Fiske_Stone_photograph_circa_1927-1932.jpg/170px-Chief_Justice_Harlan_Fiske_Stone_photograph_circa_1927-1932.jpg" width="170" height="216" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Chief_Justice_Harlan_Fiske_Stone_photograph_circa_1927-1932.jpg/255px-Chief_Justice_Harlan_Fiske_Stone_photograph_circa_1927-1932.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Chief_Justice_Harlan_Fiske_Stone_photograph_circa_1927-1932.jpg/340px-Chief_Justice_Harlan_Fiske_Stone_photograph_circa_1927-1932.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2842" data-file-height="3606" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Chief_Justice_Harlan_Fiske_Stone_photograph_circa_1927-1932.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Coolidge appointed <a href="/wiki/Harlan_Fiske_Stone" title="Harlan Fiske Stone" class="mw-redirect">Harlan Fiske Stone</a> Attorney General and then Supreme Court Justice</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Coolidge appointed one justice to the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_the_United_States" title="Supreme Court of the United States">Supreme Court of the United States</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harlan_Fiske_Stone" title="Harlan Fiske Stone" class="mw-redirect">Harlan Fiske Stone</a> in 1925. Stone was Coolidge's fellow Amherst alumnus, a Wall Street lawyer and conservative Republican. Stone was serving as dean of <a href="/wiki/Columbia_Law_School" title="Columbia Law School">Columbia Law School</a> when Coolidge appointed him to be attorney general in 1924 to restore the reputation tarnished by Harding's Attorney General, <a href="/wiki/Harry_M._Daugherty" title="Harry M. Daugherty">Harry M. Daugherty</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess364_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess364-156"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup> Stone proved to be a firm believer in <a href="/wiki/Judicial_restraint" title="Judicial restraint">judicial restraint</a> and was regarded as one of the court's <a href="/wiki/The_Three_Musketeers_(Supreme_Court)" title="The Three Musketeers (Supreme Court)">three liberal justices</a> who would often vote to uphold New Deal legislation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGalston.27.27passim.27.27_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGalston.27.27passim.27.27-157"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a></sup> President <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> later appointed Stone to be chief justice.</p>
<p>Coolidge nominated 17 judges to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Courts_of_Appeals" title="United States Courts of Appeals" class="mw-redirect">United States Courts of Appeals</a>, and 61 judges to the <a href="/wiki/United_States_district_courts" title="United States district courts" class="mw-redirect">United States district courts</a>. He appointed judges to various specialty courts as well, including <a href="/wiki/Genevieve_R._Cline" title="Genevieve R. Cline">Genevieve R. Cline</a>, who became the first woman named to the federal judiciary when Coolidge placed her on the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Customs_Court" title="United States Customs Court" class="mw-redirect">United States Customs Court</a> in 1928.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFreeman216_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreeman216-158"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup> Coolidge also signed the <a href="/wiki/Judiciary_Act_of_1925" title="Judiciary Act of 1925">Judiciary Act of 1925</a> into law, allowing the Supreme Court more discretion over its workload.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Retirement_and_death">Retirement and death</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Retirement and death">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coolidge_public_address.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Coolidge_public_address.jpg/220px-Coolidge_public_address.jpg" width="220" height="163" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Coolidge_public_address.jpg/330px-Coolidge_public_address.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c3/Coolidge_public_address.jpg/440px-Coolidge_public_address.jpg 2x" data-file-width="861" data-file-height="637" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coolidge_public_address.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Coolidge addressing a crowd at <a href="/wiki/Arlington_National_Cemetery" title="Arlington National Cemetery">Arlington National Cemetery</a>'s Roman style <a href="/wiki/Arlington_Memorial_Amphitheater" title="Arlington Memorial Amphitheater">Memorial Amphitheater</a> in 1924.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After his presidency, Coolidge retired to the modest rented house on residential Massasoit Street in <a href="/wiki/Northampton,_Massachusetts" title="Northampton, Massachusetts">Northampton</a> before moving to a more spacious home, "The Beeches."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel407_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel407-159"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a></sup> He kept a <a href="/wiki/Hacker-Craft" title="Hacker-Craft">Hacker</a> runabout boat on the Connecticut River and was often observed on the water by local boating enthusiasts. During this period, he also served as chairman of the non-partisan Railroad Commission, as honorary president of the <a href="/wiki/American_Foundation_for_the_Blind" title="American Foundation for the Blind">American Foundation for the Blind</a>, as a director of <a href="/wiki/New_York_Life_Insurance_Company" title="New York Life Insurance Company">New York Life Insurance Company</a>, as president of the <a href="/wiki/American_Antiquarian_Society" title="American Antiquarian Society">American Antiquarian Society</a>, and as a trustee of Amherst College.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess450.E2.80.9355_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess450.E2.80.9355-160"><span>[</span>156<span>]</span></a></sup> Coolidge received an honorary <a href="/wiki/Doctor_of_Laws" title="Doctor of Laws" class="mw-redirect">Doctor of Laws</a> degree from <a href="/wiki/Bates_College" title="Bates College">Bates College</a> in <a href="/wiki/Lewiston,_Maine" title="Lewiston, Maine">Lewiston, Maine</a>.</p>
<p>Coolidge published <a href="/wiki/The_Autobiography_of_Calvin_Coolidge" title="The Autobiography of Calvin Coolidge">his autobiography</a> in 1929 and wrote a syndicated newspaper column, "Calvin Coolidge Says," from 1930 to 1931.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel403Ferrell201.E2.80.9302_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel403Ferrell201.E2.80.9302-161"><span>[</span>157<span>]</span></a></sup> Faced with looming defeat in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_presidential_election,_1932" title="United States presidential election, 1932">1932 presidential election</a>, some Republicans spoke of rejecting Herbert Hoover as their party's nominee, and instead drafting Coolidge to run, but the former President made it clear that he was not interested in running again, and that he would publicly repudiate any effort to draft him, should it come about.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess457.E2.80.9359Greenberg153_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess457.E2.80.9359Greenberg153-162"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup> Hoover was renominated, and Coolidge made several radio addresses in support of him. Hoover then lost the general election to Democrat <a href="/wiki/Franklin_D._Roosevelt" title="Franklin D. Roosevelt">Franklin D. Roosevelt</a> in a landslide.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFuess460_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFuess460-163"><span>[</span>159<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>He died suddenly from <a href="/wiki/Coronary_thrombosis" title="Coronary thrombosis">coronary thrombosis</a> at "The Beeches," at 12:45&#160;p.m., January 5, 1933.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenberg154.E2.80.9355_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenberg154.E2.80.9355-164"><span>[</span>160<span>]</span></a></sup> Shortly before his death, Coolidge confided to an old friend: "I feel I no longer fit in with these times."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel410_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel410-165"><span>[</span>161<span>]</span></a></sup> Coolidge is buried beneath a simple headstone in <a href="/wiki/Plymouth_Notch_Cemetery" title="Plymouth Notch Cemetery">Plymouth Notch Cemetery</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plymouth_Notch" title="Plymouth Notch" class="mw-redirect">Plymouth Notch</a>, Vermont, where the nearby family home is maintained as one of the original buildings on the <a href="/wiki/Calvin_Coolidge_Homestead_District" title="Calvin Coolidge Homestead District" class="mw-redirect">Calvin Coolidge Homestead District</a> site. The State of Vermont dedicated a new visitors' center nearby to mark Coolidge's 100th birthday on July 4, 1972.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Radio.2C_film.2C_and_commemorations">Radio, film, and commemorations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Radio, film, and commemorations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coolidge_with_press.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Coolidge_with_press.jpg/170px-Coolidge_with_press.jpg" width="170" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Coolidge_with_press.jpg/255px-Coolidge_with_press.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Coolidge_with_press.jpg/340px-Coolidge_with_press.jpg 2x" data-file-width="478" data-file-height="597" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coolidge_with_press.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Coolidge, reporters, and cameramen</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Despite his reputation as a quiet and even reclusive politician, Coolidge made use of the new medium of radio and made radio history several times while President. He made himself available to reporters, giving 520 press conferences, meeting with reporters more regularly than any President before or since.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGreenberg7_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGreenberg7-166"><span>[</span>162<span>]</span></a></sup> Coolidge's <a href="/wiki/Second_inauguration_of_Calvin_Coolidge" title="Second inauguration of Calvin Coolidge">second inauguration</a> was the first presidential inauguration broadcast on radio. On December 6, 1923, he was the first President whose address to Congress was broadcast on radio.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESobel252_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESobel252-167"><span>[</span>163<span>]</span></a></sup> Coolidge signed the <a href="/wiki/Radio_Act_of_1927" title="Radio Act of 1927" class="mw-redirect">Radio Act of 1927</a>, which assigned regulation of radio to the newly created <a href="/wiki/Federal_Radio_Commission" title="Federal Radio Commission">Federal Radio Commission</a>. On August 11, 1924, <a href="/wiki/Theodore_W._Case" title="Theodore W. Case" class="mw-redirect">Theodore W. Case</a>, using the <a href="/wiki/Phonofilm" title="Phonofilm">Phonofilm</a> <a href="/wiki/Sound-on-film" title="Sound-on-film">sound-on-film</a> process he developed for <a href="/wiki/Lee_DeForest" title="Lee DeForest" class="mw-redirect">Lee DeForest</a>, filmed Coolidge on the White House lawn, making Coolidge the first President to appear in a sound film. The title of the DeForest film was <i>President Coolidge, Taken on the White House Grounds</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup> When <a href="/wiki/Charles_Lindbergh" title="Charles Lindbergh">Charles Lindbergh</a> arrived in Washington on a U.S. navy ship after his celebrated 1927 trans-Atlantic flight, President Coolidge welcomed him back to the U.S. and a sound-on-film record of the event exists. Coolidge was the only president to have his portrait on a coin during his lifetime, the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Sesquicentennial_coinage" title="United States Sesquicentennial coinage">Sesquicentennial of American Independence Half Dollar</a>, minted in 1926. After his death he also appeared on a <a href="/wiki/US_Presidents_on_US_postage_stamps#Calvin_Coolidge" title="US Presidents on US postage stamps" class="mw-redirect">postage stamp</a>, pictured below.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>U.S. President Ronald W. Reagan regarded Coolidge as his favorite 20th century U.S. president because of Coolidge's belief in a more limited U.S. federal government. Reagan cited Coolidge's tough handling of striking Boston police when he fired striking air traffic controllers in 1981. Coolidge's reputation remains strong among most political conservatives.</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-traditional">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
<div style="width: 155px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;">
<div style="margin:17px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sesquicentennial_american_independence_half_dollar_commemorative_obverse.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Sesquicentennial_american_independence_half_dollar_commemorative_obverse.jpg/120px-Sesquicentennial_american_independence_half_dollar_commemorative_obverse.jpg" width="120" height="116" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Sesquicentennial_american_independence_half_dollar_commemorative_obverse.jpg/180px-Sesquicentennial_american_independence_half_dollar_commemorative_obverse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Sesquicentennial_american_independence_half_dollar_commemorative_obverse.jpg/240px-Sesquicentennial_american_independence_half_dollar_commemorative_obverse.jpg 2x" data-file-width="268" data-file-height="259" /></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Coolidge and Washington on the Sesquicentennial of American Independence commemorative half-dollar</p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 155px">
<div style="width: 155px">
<div class="thumb" style="width: 150px;">
<div style="margin:15px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Calvin_Coolidge_1938_Issue-$5.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Calvin_Coolidge_1938_Issue-%245.jpg/106px-Calvin_Coolidge_1938_Issue-%245.jpg" width="106" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Calvin_Coolidge_1938_Issue-%245.jpg/159px-Calvin_Coolidge_1938_Issue-%245.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Calvin_Coolidge_1938_Issue-%245.jpg/211px-Calvin_Coolidge_1938_Issue-%245.jpg 2x" data-file-width="511" data-file-height="580" /></a></div>
</div>
<div class="gallerytext">
<p>Coolidge on a 1938 postage stamp</p>
</div>
</div>
</li>
</ul>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Coolidge_effect" title="Coolidge effect">Coolidge effect</a>, whereby males (and to a lesser extent females) exhibit renewed sexual interest if introduced to new receptive sexual partners, even after refusing sex from prior but still available sexual partners, is named after the former President. Behavioral endocrinologist Frank A. Beach first mentioned the term "Coolidge effect" in publication in 1955, crediting one of his students with suggesting the term at a psychology conference.<sup id="cite_ref-Dewsbury_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dewsbury-169"><span>[</span>165<span>]</span></a></sup> He attributed the neologism to:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>an old joke about Calvin Coolidge when he was President … The President and Mrs. Coolidge were being shown (separately) around an experimental government farm. When [Mrs. Coolidge] came to the chicken yard she noticed that a rooster was mating very frequently. She asked the attendant how often that happened and was told, "Dozens of times each day." Mrs. Coolidge said, "Tell that to the President when he comes by." Upon being told, the President asked, "Same hen every time?" The reply was, "Oh, no, Mr. President, a different hen every time." President: "Tell that to Mrs. Coolidge."</p>
<div class="templatequotecite"><cite>—&#160;<i>The joke appears in a 1978 book (A New Look at Love, by Elaine Hatfield and G. William Walster, p. 75), citing an earlier source (footnote 19, Chapter 5).<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170"><span>[</span>166<span>]</span></a></sup></i></cite></div>
</blockquote>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Calvin_Coolidge&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-count references-column-count-4" style="-moz-column-count: 4; -webkit-column-count: 4; column-count: 4; list-style-type: decimal;">

<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCoy420.E2.80.9321Greenberg49.E2.80.9353-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcCoy420.E2.80.9321Greenberg49.E2.80.9353_1-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFMcCoy">McCoy</a>, pp.&#160;420–21; <a href="#CITEREFGreenberg">Greenberg</a>, pp.&#160;49–53.</span></li>
